932 <section id="_1">

933

934 <h2>

935General Information About Hodgkin Lymphoma (HL)</h2>

936

937 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

938<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

939<h6>

940In This Section</h6>

941<ul>

942<li>

943<a href="#_470_toc">

944Incidence and Mortality</a>

945</li>

946<li>

947<a href="#_721_toc">

948Anatomy </a>

949</li>

950<li>

951<a href="#_723_toc">

952Risk Factors </a>

953</li>

954<li>

955<a href="#_726_toc">

956Clinical Features</a>

957</li>

958<li>

959<a href="#_730_toc">

960Diagnostic Evaluation</a>

961</li>

962<li>

963<a href="#_645_toc">

964Prognostic Factors</a>

965</li>

966<li>

967<a href="#_970_toc">

968Follow-Up</a>

969</li>

970<li>

971<a href="#_757_toc">

972Adverse Long-Term Effects of Therapy <ul>

973<li>

974<a href="#_886_toc">

975Second malignancies</a>

976</li>

977<li>

978<a href="#_867_toc">

979Other adverse long-term effects </a>

980</li>

981</ul>

982</a>

983</li>

984</ul>

985</nav>

986<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

987<a href="/types/lymphoma/patient/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq">

988Go to Patient Version</a>

989</div>

990<section id="_470">

991<h3 id="_470_toc">

992Incidence and Mortality</h3>

993<p id="_331" tabindex="-1">

994Estimated new cases and deaths from HL in the United States in 2023:[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9951</a>

996]</p>

997<div class="pdq-content-list">

998<ul id="_332">

999<li>

1000New cases: 8,830.</li>

1001<li>

1002Deaths: 900.</li>

1003</ul>

1004</div>

1005<p id="_3" tabindex="-1">

1006Up to 90% of all newly diagnosed patients with HL can be cured with combination chemotherapy and/or radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

10072</a>

1008] </p>

1009</section>

1010<section id="_721">

1011<h3 id="_721_toc">

1012Anatomy </h3>

1013<figure id="figure_722" class="image-center-medium">

1014<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/533339.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1015Enlarge</a>

1016<img id="_722" alt="Lymph system; drawing shows the lymph vessels and lymph organs including the lymph nodes, tonsils, thymus, spleen, and bone marrow. One inset shows the inside structure of a lymph node and the attached lymph vessels with arrows showing how the lymph (clear fluid) moves into and out of the lymph node. Another inset shows a close up of bone marrow with blood cells." title="Lymph system; drawing shows the lymph vessels and lymph organs including the lymph nodes, tonsils, thymus, spleen, and bone marrow. One inset shows the inside structure of a lymph node and the attached lymph vessels with arrows showing how the lymph (clear fluid) moves into and out of the lymph node. Another inset shows a close up of bone marrow with blood cells." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/533339-750.jpg">

1017<figcaption class="caption-container">

1018Anatomy of the lymph system.</figcaption>

1019</figure>

1020<p id="_996" tabindex="-1">

1021HL most frequently presents in lymph node groups above the diaphragm and/or in mediastinal lymph nodes. Involvement of Waldeyer's ring or tonsillar lymph glands is rarely seen.</p>

1022</section>

1023<section id="_723">

1024<h3 id="_723_toc">

1025Risk Factors </h3>

1026<p id="_724" tabindex="-1">

1027Risk factors for HL include the following:</p>

1028<div class="pdq-content-list">

1029<ul id="_725">

1030<li>

1031Being in early adulthood (aged 20&#8211;39 years) (most often) or late adulthood (aged 65 years and older) (less often).</li>

1032<li>

1033Being male.</li>

1034<li>

1035Having a previous infection with the Epstein-Barr virus in the teenage years or early childhood.</li>

1036<li>

1037Having a first-degree relative with HL.</li>

1038</ul>

1039</div>

1040</section>

1041<section id="_726">

1042<h3 id="_726_toc">

1043Clinical Features</h3>

1044<p id="_727" tabindex="-1">

1045These and other signs and symptoms may be caused by HL or by other conditions:</p>

1046<div class="pdq-content-list">

1047<ul id="_728">

1048<li>

1049Painless, swollen lymph nodes in the neck, axilla, or inguinal area.</li>

1050<li>

1051Fever defined as 38&#186;C or higher.</li>

1052<li>

1053Drenching and recurrent night sweats.</li>

1054<li>

1055Weight loss of 10% or more of baseline weight in the previous 6 months.</li>

1056<li>

1057Pruritus, especially after bathing or after ingesting alcohol.</li>

1058<li>

1059Fatigue.</li>

1060</ul>

1061</div>

1062<p id="_729" tabindex="-1">

1063Treatment of HL should relieve these symptoms within days. For more information, see <a href="/about-cancer/treatment/side-effects/hot-flashes-hp-pdq">

1064Hot Flashes and Night Sweats</a>

1065, <a href="/about-cancer/treatment/side-effects/skin-nail-changes/pruritus-hp-pdq">

1066Pruritus</a>

1067, and <a href="/about-cancer/treatment/side-effects/fatigue/fatigue-hp-pdq">

1068Fatigue</a>

1069.</p>

1070</section>

1071<section id="_730">

1072<h3 id="_730_toc">

1073Diagnostic Evaluation</h3>

1074<p id="_917" tabindex="-1">

1075Diagnostic evaluation of patients with lymphoma may include the following:</p>

1076<div class="pdq-content-list">

1077<ol id="_918">

1078<li>

1079Biopsy (preferably excisional), with interpretation by a qualified pathologist.</li>

1080<li>

1081History, with special attention given to the presence and duration of fever, night sweats, and unexplained weight loss of 10% or more of body weight in the previous 6 months.</li>

1082<li>

1083Physical examination.</li>

1084<li>

1085Laboratory tests.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1086<ul id="_919">

1087<li>

1088Complete blood cell count and platelet count.</li>

1089<li>

1090Erythrocyte sedimentation rate.</li>

1091<li>

1092Chemistry panel (electrolytes, blood urea nitrogen, creatinine, calcium, aspartate transaminase, alanine aminotransferase, bilirubin, and alkaline phosphatase) plus lactate dehydrogenase, uric acid, and phosphorus.</li>

1093</ul>

1094</div>

1095</li>

1096<li>

1097Radiographic examination.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1098<ul id="_920">

1099<li>

1100Computed tomography (CT) of the neck, chest, abdomen, and pelvis; or metabolic imaging (fluorine F 18-fludeoxyglucose positron emission tomography [PET]) with PET-CT. PET-magnetic resonance imaging scans may be equivalent to PET-CT in obtaining staging information at 25% of the radiation dose.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

11013</a>

1102]</li>

1103</ul>

1104</div>

1105</li>

1106<li>

1107HIV testing.</li>

1108<li>

1109Hepatitis B and hepatitis C serology.</li>

1110</ol>

1111</div>

1112<p id="_966" tabindex="-1">

1113All stages of HL can be subclassified into A and B categories: B for those with defined general symptoms (described below) and A for those without B symptoms. The B designation is given to patients with any of the following symptoms:</p>

1114<div class="pdq-content-list">

1115<ul id="_967">

1116<li>

1117Unexplained weight loss (more than 10% of body weight in the 6 months before diagnosis).</li>

1118<li>

1119Unexplained fever with temperatures above 38&#176;C.</li>

1120<li>

1121Drenching and recurrent night sweats.</li>

1122</ul>

1123</div>

1124<p id="_968" tabindex="-1">

1125The most significant B symptoms are fevers and weight loss. Night sweats alone do not confer an adverse prognosis.</p>

1126</section>

1127<section id="_645">

1128<h3 id="_645_toc">

1129Prognostic Factors</h3>

1130<p id="_731" tabindex="-1">

1131The prognosis for a given patient depends on several factors. The most important

1132factors are the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

11331</a>

1134,<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

11354</a>

1136,<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

11375</a>

1138]</p>

1139<div class="pdq-content-list">

1140<ul id="_732">

1141<li>

1142Presence or absence of systemic B symptoms.</li>

1143<li>

1144Stage of disease.</li>

1145<li>

1146Presence of large masses.</li>

1147<li>

1148Quality and suitability of the treatment administered.</li>

1149</ul>

1150</div>

1151<p id="_733" tabindex="-1">

1152Other important factors are:[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

11531</a>

1154,<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

11554</a>

1156,<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

11575</a>

1158]</p>

1159<div class="pdq-content-list">

1160<ul id="_734">

1161<li>

1162Age.</li>

1163<li>

1164Sex.</li>

1165<li>

1166Erythrocyte sedimentation rate.</li>

1167<li>

1168Hematocrit.</li>

1169<li>

1170Extent of abdominal involvement.</li>

1171<li>

1172Absolute number of nodal sites of involvement.</li>

1173</ul>

1174</div>

1175<p id="_1154" tabindex="-1">

1176The best predictor of treatment failure is a PET-CT scan obtained after two cycles of chemotherapy (PET2 scan).[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11776</a>

1178,<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

11797</a>

1180] For limited-stage disease, there are frequent false-positive tests because the relapse risk is low (low-positive predictive value). For advanced-stage disease, up to 15% of patients have a relapse despite a negative PET2 scan (lowering the negative predictive value).[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11816</a>

1182,<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

11837</a>

1184] Combining biomarkers with PET-CT scanning responses or calculating metabolic tumor volume with PET-CT scanning are methods under evaluation to improve prognostic predictions.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11856</a>

1186,<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

11878</a>

1188-<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

118911</a>

1190]</p>

1191</section>

1192<section id="_970">

1193<h3 id="_970_toc">

1194Follow-Up</h3>

1195<p id="_885" tabindex="-1">

1196Recommendations for posttreatment follow-up are not evidence based, but a variety of opinions have been published for high-risk patients who present with advanced-stage disease and for patients who achieve less-than-complete remission by PET-CT scans at the end of therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

119712</a>

1198-<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

119915</a>

1200] For patients at high risk of relapse, conventional CT scans are used to avoid increased false-positive test results and increased radiation exposure of serial PET-CT scans.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

120116</a>

1202]</p>

1203<p id="_997" tabindex="-1">

1204For patients with negative findings from a PET-CT scan at the end of therapy, routine scans are not advised because of the very low risk of recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

120517</a>

1206] Opportunistic scanning is applied when patients present with suspicious symptoms, physical findings, or laboratory test results. The 5-year risk of relapse from diagnosis is 5.6% for patients remaining event-free for 2 years after induction therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

120718</a>

1208]</p>

1209<p id="_964" tabindex="-1">

1210Among 6,840 patients enrolled in German Hodgkin Study Group (GHSG) trials, with a median follow-up of 10.3 years, 141 patients had a relapse after 5 years, compared with 466 patients who had a relapse within 5 years. Treatment-related adverse effects and late relapses may occur beyond 20 years of follow-up.[<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

121119</a>

1212]</p>

1213</section>

1214<section id="_757">

1215<h3 id="_757_toc">

1216Adverse Long-Term Effects of Therapy </h3>

1217<p id="_995" tabindex="-1">

1218Patients who complete therapy for HL are at risk of developing long-term side effects, ranging from direct damage to organ function or the immune system to second malignancies. For the first 15 years after treatment, HL is the main cause of death. By 15 to 20 years after therapy, the cumulative mortality from a second malignancy, cardiovascular disease, or pulmonary fibrosis exceeds the cumulative mortality from HL.[<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

121920</a>

1220-<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

122123</a>

1222] This risk of developing a second malignancy is even higher for individuals with a family history of cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

122324</a>

1224]</p>

1225<p id="_1310" tabindex="-1">

1226Compared with the general population, long-term survivors of HL have a significantly lower life expectancy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

122725</a>

1228] A multicenter cohort study of 4,919 patients treated between 1965 and 2000 and before age 51 years had a median follow-up of 20.2 years. Patients with HL had an absolute excess mortality (AEM) of 123 excess deaths per 10,000 person-years. This risk (standardized mortality ratio, 5.2; 95% confidence interval [CI], 4.2&#8211;6.5; AEM, 619) was maintained for 40-year survivors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

122925</a>

1230] For example, at age 54 years, the cumulative mortality of 20.0% for HL survivors was commensurate with that of a 71-year-old person from the general population. While mortality from HL dropped precipitously from 1965 to 2000, solid tumor mortality did not change over that time.[<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

123125</a>

1232]</p>

1233<section id="_886">

1234<h4 id="_886_toc">

1235Second malignancies</h4>

1236<p id="_945" tabindex="-1">

1237Recommendations for screening for secondary malignancies or follow-up of long-term survivors are consensus based and not derived from randomized trials.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

123826</a>

1239]</p>

1240<section id="_889">

1241<h5 id="_889_toc">

1242Solid tumors</h5>

1243<p id="_769" tabindex="-1">

1244 An increase in second solid tumors has also been observed,

1245especially mesothelioma and cancers of the lung, breast, thyroid, bone/soft tissue, stomach, esophagus, colon and rectum, uterine cervix, and head and neck.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

124627</a>

1247-<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

124833</a>

1249]

1250These tumors occur primarily after radiation therapy or with combined-modality

1251treatment, and approximately 75% occur within radiation ports. The risk of developing a second solid tumor (cumulative incidence of a second cancer) increases with time after treatment.</p>

1252<div class="pdq-content-list">

1253<ul id="_947">

1254<li>

1255At 15-years of

1256follow-up, the risk is approximately 13%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

125730</a>

1258] </li>

1259<li>

1260At 20-years of follow-up, the risk is approximately 17%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

126134</a>

1262] </li>

1263<li>

1264At 25-years of follow-up, the risk is approximately 22%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

126527</a>

1266,<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

126735</a>

1268] </li>

1269<li>

1270At 40-years of follow-up, the risk is approximately 48%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

127136</a>

1272]</li>

1273</ul>

1274</div>

1275<p id="_890" tabindex="-1">

1276In a cohort of 18,862 5-year survivors from 13 population-based registries, the younger patients had elevated risks for breast, colon, and rectal cancers for 10 to 25 years before the ages when routine screening is recommended in the general population.[<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

127729</a>

1278] Even with involved-field doses of 15 Gy to 25 Gy, sarcomas, breast cancers, and thyroid cancers occurred with similar incidence in young patients, compared with those receiving higher-dose radiation.[<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

127934</a>

1280]</p>

1281<p id="_948" tabindex="-1">

1282Lung cancer and breast cancer are among the most-common second solid tumors that develop after therapy for HL.</p>

1283<div class="pdq-content-list">

1284<ul id="_770">

1285<li>

1286<strong>

1287Lung cancer.</strong>

1288 Lung

1289cancer is seen with increased frequency, even after chemotherapy alone, and the

1290risk of this cancer increases with cigarette smoking.[<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

129137</a>

1292-<a href="#cit/section_1.40">

129340</a>

1294] In a retrospective Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program analysis, stage-specific survival was decreased by 30% to 60% in HL survivors, compared with patients with de novo non-small cell lung cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.41">

129541</a>

1296] </li>

1297<li>

1298<strong>

1299Breast cancer.</strong>

1300 Breast cancer

1301is seen with increased frequency after radiation therapy or combined-modality

1302therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

130327</a>

1304,<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

130528</a>

1306,<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

130742</a>

1308-<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

130944</a>

1310] The risk appears greatest for females treated with radiation

1311before age 30 years, especially for girls close to menarche.[<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

131245</a>

1313] The incidence of breast cancer increases substantially after 15 years

1314of posttherapy follow-up.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

131527</a>

1316,<a href="#cit/section_1.46">

131746</a>

1318,<a href="#cit/section_1.47">

131947</a>

1320]

1321<p id="_891" tabindex="-1">

1322In two case-control studies of 479 patients who developed breast cancer after therapy for HL, cumulative absolute risks for developing breast cancer were calculated as a function of radiation therapy dose and the use of chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.48">

132348</a>

1324,<a href="#cit/section_1.49">

132549</a>

1326] With a 30-year to 40-year follow-up, cumulative absolute risks of breast cancer with exposure to radiation range from 8.5% to 39.6%, depending on age at diagnosis. These cohort studies show a continued increase in cumulative excess risk of breast cancer beyond 20 years of follow-up.[<a href="#cit/section_1.48">

132748</a>

1328,<a href="#cit/section_1.49">

132949</a>

1330]</p>

1331<p id="_771" tabindex="-1">

1332In a nested case-control study and subsequent cohort study, patients who received both chemotherapy and radiation therapy had a statistically significant lower risk of developing breast cancer than did those treated with radiation therapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

133342</a>

1334,<a href="#cit/section_1.50">

133550</a>

1336] Reaching early menopause with fewer than 10 years of intact ovarian function appeared to account for the reduction in risk among patients who received combined-modality therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.50">

133750</a>

1338] Reduction of radiation volume also decreased the risk of breast cancer after HL.[<a href="#cit/section_1.50">

133950</a>

1340] </p>

1341</li>

1342</ul>

1343</div>

1344<p id="_773" tabindex="-1">

1345Late effects of autologous stem cell transplant for failure of induction chemotherapy include second malignancies, hypothyroidism, hypogonadism, herpes zoster, depression, and cardiac disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.51">

134651</a>

1347]</p>

1348</section>

1349<section id="_1311">

1350<h5 id="_1311_toc">

1351Hematologic cancers</h5>

1352<div class="pdq-content-list">

1353<ul id="_1312">

1354<li>

1355<strong>

1356Acute myelogenous leukemia (AML).</strong>

1357 Acute nonlymphocytic leukemia may occur in patients treated with combined-modality therapy or with combination chemotherapy alone, especially with increased exposure to alkylating agents.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

135830</a>

1359,<a href="#cit/section_1.52">

136052</a>

1361]<div class="pdq-content-list">

1362<ul id="_1313">

1363<li>

1364At 10 years after therapy with regimens containing MOPP (mechlorethamine, vincristine, procarbazine, and prednisone), the risk of AML is approximately 3%, with the peak incidence occurring 5 to 9 years after therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

136530</a>

1366,<a href="#cit/section_1.52">

136752</a>

1368] The risk of acute leukemia at 10 years after therapy with ABVD (doxorubicin, bleomycin, vinblastine, and dacarbazine) appears to be less than 1%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.53">

136953</a>

1370] </li>

1371<li>

1372A population-based study of more than 35,000 survivors during a 30-year time span identified 217 patients who developed AML. The excess absolute risk (EAR) was significantly higher for older patients (&gt;35 years at diagnosis) than for younger survivors (EAR, 9.9 vs. 4.2 per 10,000 patient-years, <em>

1373P</em>

1374 &lt; .001).[<a href="#cit/section_1.54">

137554</a>

1376]</li>

1377</ul>

1378</div>

1379</li>

1380</ul>

1381</div>

1382</section>

1383</section>

1384<section id="_867">

1385<h4 id="_867_toc">

1386Other adverse long-term effects </h4>

1387<p id="_949" tabindex="-1">

1388Treatment of HL also affects the endocrine, cardiac, pulmonary, skeletal, and immune systems. Chronic fatigue can be a debilitating symptom for some long-term survivors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.55">

138955</a>

1390] A retrospective survey of 20,007 patients with early- and advanced-stage classical HL treated between 2000 and 2016 (i.e., the era in which ABVD became the preferred frontline chemotherapy regimen) showed 1,321 deaths not attributable to lymphoma (39% of total deaths). Heart disease (estimated EAR as follows: 6.6 per 10,000 patient-years, standardized mortality ratio, 1.7 for early-stage disease and 15.1 per 10,000 patient-years, standardized mortality ratio, 2.1 for advanced-stage disease) and infection (estimated EAR as follows: 3.1 per 10,000 patient-years, standardized mortality ratio, 2.2 for early-stage disease and 10.6 per 10,000 patient-years, standardized mortality ratio, 3.9 for advanced-stage disease) were the leading causes of death, especially in patients older than 60 years.[<a href="#cit/section_1.56">

139156</a>

1392]</p>

1393<p id="_760" tabindex="-1">

1394<strong>

1395Infertility.</strong>

1396 A toxic effect that is primarily related to chemotherapy is infertility, usually after regimens containing MOPP or BEACOPP (bleomycin, etoposide, doxorubicin, cyclophosphamide, vincristine, procarbazine, and prednisone).[<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

139757</a>

1398-<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

139959</a>

1400] After six to eight cycles of BEACOPP, most men had testosterone levels within reference range; however, 82% of women younger than 30 years recovered menses (mostly within 12 months), but only 45% of women older than 30 years recovered menses.[<a href="#cit/section_1.60">

140160</a>

1402] ABVD appears to spare long-term testicular and ovarian function.[<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

140358</a>

1404,<a href="#cit/section_1.61">

140561</a>

1406,<a href="#cit/section_1.62">

140762</a>

1408] Increasing age and alkylator-based regimens are the two major factors increasing the risk of premature ovarian insufficiency.[<a href="#cit/section_1.60">

140960</a>

1410,<a href="#cit/section_1.63">

141163</a>

1412,<a href="#cit/section_1.64">

141364</a>

1414] A prospective evaluation of gonadal function embedded in the randomized Response-Adapted Therapy in Advanced Hodgkin Lymphoma (RATHL) study for patients with newly diagnosed advanced-stage HL found good recovery of anti-M&#252;llerian hormone concentration and reduction in follicle-stimulating hormone after ABVD or AVD (doxorubicin, vinblastine, dacarbazine), but less recovery after BEACOPP and for women older than 35 years.[<a href="#cit/section_1.63">

141563</a>

1416] A PET scan-adapted treatment regimen to reduce the use of BEACOPP also resulted in less infertility and gonadal dysfunction.[<a href="#cit/section_1.65">

141765</a>

1418] While cryopreservation of oocytes or sperm remains the first choice for preservation of fertility, luteinizing hormone-releasing hormone agonists can be tried in this setting, although efficacy for patients with HL has not been confirmed as has been confirmed for patients with breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.66">

141966</a>

1420] A national Danish registry of 793 HL survivors showed that patients who did not have a relapse had similar parenthood rates to the general population, but assistive reproduction methods were required more often for HL survivors (male, 21.6% vs. 6.3%; female, 13.6% vs. 5.5%; <em>

1421P</em>

1422 &#8804; .001 for both comparisons).[<a href="#cit/section_1.67">

142367</a>

1424]</p>

1425<p id="_761" tabindex="-1">

1426<strong>

1427Hypothyroidism.</strong>

1428 Hypothyroidism is a late complication primarily related to radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.68">

142968</a>

1430-<a href="#cit/section_1.70">

143170</a>

1432] Long-term survivors who receive radiation therapy to the neck are followed up with annual thyroid-stimulating hormone testing.</p>

1433<p id="_762" tabindex="-1">

1434<strong>

1435Cardiac disease.</strong>

1436 A late complication primarily related to

1437radiation therapy is cardiac disease, the risk of which may persist for over 30 years after the first treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_1.68">

143868</a>

1439,<a href="#cit/section_1.71">

144071</a>

1441-<a href="#cit/section_1.79">

144279</a>

1443] The EAR of fatal cardiovascular disease ranges from 11.9 to 48.9 per 10,000 patient-years and is mostly attributable to fatal myocardial infarction (MI).[<a href="#cit/section_1.71">

144471</a>

1445-<a href="#cit/section_1.73">

144673</a>

1447,<a href="#cit/section_1.75">

144875</a>

1449] A retrospective survey of over 6,000 patients with HL treated in trials between 1964 and 2004 found that cardiac exposure to radiation and use of doxorubicin were significant predictors of ischemic heart disease, congestive heart failure, arrhythmias, and vascular disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.77">

145077</a>

1451] In a cohort of 7,033 patients with HL, MI mortality risk persisted for 25 years after first treatment with supradiaphragmatic radiation therapy (dependent on the details of treatment planning), doxorubicin, or vincristine.[<a href="#cit/section_1.75">

145275</a>

1453,<a href="#cit/section_1.76">

145476</a>

1455] A nested case-control study of 2,617 5-year survivors of HL diagnosed before age 51 years and treated between 1965 and 1995 found that the 25-year risk of moderate to severe heart failure increased for patients receiving anthracyclines. The risk ranged from 11.2% for patients exposed to 0 Gy to 15 Gy radiation up to 32.9% for patients exposed to radiation equal or greater than 21 Gy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.80">

145680</a>

1457] The use of subcranial blocking did not reduce the incidence of fatal MI in a retrospective review, perhaps because of the exposure of the proximal coronary arteries to radiation.[<a href="#cit/section_1.72">

145872</a>

1459] Compared with a general matched population, HL patients treated with mediastinal radiation were at increased risk of complications, especially during cardiac surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_1.81">

146081</a>

1461] Risk prediction models rely on the dose of mediastinal radiation, smoking history, male sex, and anthracycline exposure to define the patients at highest risk.[<a href="#cit/section_1.79">

146279</a>

1463] These risk prediction models found that mediastinal radiation therapy combined with doxorubicin exposure conferred the highest risk, followed by mediastinal radiation therapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_1.79">

146479</a>

1465]</p>

1466<p id="_1266" tabindex="-1">

1467In the U.K. RAPID trial, performed between 2003 and 2010, 183 patients with early-stage HL were PET-negative but still received involved-field radiation therapy (IFRT) (20 Gy) after receiving ABVD.[<a href="#cit/section_1.78">

146878</a>

1469] The average predicted 30-year cardiovascular mortality was 5.02%, which included 3.52% expected in the general population, 0.94% EAR from the doxorubicin, and 0.56% from the IFRT. Since 2010, radiation therapy techniques have advanced by using smaller target volumes, lower-dose IFRT (20 Gy), deep inspiration breath holding, intensity-modulated radiation therapy, and proton beam therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.78">

147078</a>

1471] These techniques will need further evaluation to better assess cardiovascular risks from radiation therapy.</p>

1472<p id="_763" tabindex="-1">

1473<strong>

1474Pulmonary impairment.</strong>

1475 Impairment of

1476pulmonary function may occur as a result of mantle-field radiation therapy; this

1477impairment is not usually clinically evident, and recovery in pulmonary testing

1478often occurs after 2 to 3 years.[<a href="#cit/section_1.82">

147982</a>

1480] Pulmonary toxic effects from bleomycin as used in ABVD are seen in patients older than 40 years.[<a href="#cit/section_1.83">

148183</a>

1482] </p>

1483<p id="_764" tabindex="-1">

1484<strong>

1485Bone necrosis.</strong>

1486 Avascular necrosis of bone has

1487been observed in patients treated with chemotherapy and is most likely related

1488to corticosteroid therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.84">

148984</a>

1490]</p>

1491<p id="_765" tabindex="-1">

1492<strong>

1493Bacterial sepsis.</strong>

1494 Bacterial sepsis may occur rarely after

1495splenectomy performed during staging laparotomy for HL;[<a href="#cit/section_1.85">

149685</a>

1497] it

1498is much more common in children than in adults. </p>

1499<p id="_766" tabindex="-1">

1500<strong>

1501Fatigue.</strong>

1502 Fatigue is a commonly reported symptom among patients who have completed

1503chemotherapy and radiation therapy. In a case-control study design, most HL

1504survivors reported significant fatigue lasting for more than 6 months after

1505therapy, compared with age-matched controls. Quality-of-life questionnaires given to 5,306 patients on GHSG trials showed that 20% of patients complained of severe fatigue 5 years after therapy, and those patients had significantly increased problems with employment and financial stability.[<a href="#cit/section_1.86">

150686</a>

1507-<a href="#cit/section_1.88">

150888</a>

1509]

1510For more information, see <a href="/about-cancer/treatment/side-effects/fatigue/fatigue-hp-pdq">

1511Fatigue</a>

1512.</p>

1513<p id="_1314" tabindex="-1">

1514<strong>

1515Neurocognitive impairment.</strong>

1516 After a median of 23 years from diagnosis, 1,760 HL survivors treated in childhood were compared with 3,180 siblings. Significantly higher rates of memory loss (8.1% vs. 5.7%; <em>

1517P</em>

1518 &lt; .05), anxiety (7.0% vs. 5.4%; <em>

1519P</em>

1520 &lt; .05), unemployment (9.6% vs. 4.4%; <em>

1521P</em>

1522 &lt; .05), depression (9.1% vs. 7.0%; <em>

1523P</em>

1524 &lt; .05), and impaired physical quality of life (11.2% vs. 3.0%; <em>

1525P</em>

1526 &lt; .05) were reported.[<a href="#cit/section_1.89">

152789</a>

1528] Lower risks were associated with survivors who adhered to exercise guidelines and did not smoke, but the design of this study did not allow a cause-and-effect conclusion.</p>

1529</section>

1530</section>

1875Loge JH, Abrahamsen AF, Ekeberg O, et al.: Hodgkin's disease survivors more fatigued than the general population. J Clin Oncol 17 (1): 253-61, 1999.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=10458240&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=10458240&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1876[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1877</li>

1878<li id="section_1.88">

1879Kreissl S, Mueller H, Goergen H, et al.: Cancer-related fatigue in patients with and survivors of Hodgkin's lymphoma: a longitudinal study of the German Hodgkin Study Group. Lancet Oncol 17 (10): 1453-1462, 2016.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27612583&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27612583&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1880[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1884[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1885</li>

1886</ol>

1887</div>

1895 <h2>

1896Cellular Classification of HL</h2>

1897

1898 <div id="_section_295" class="pdq-sections">

1899<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1900<h6>

1901In This Section</h6>

1902<ul>

1903<li>

1904<a href="#_179_toc">

1905WHO Modification of the REAL Classification</a>

1906</li>

1907</ul>

1908</nav>

1909<p id="_6" tabindex="-1">

1910Pathologists currently use the World Health Organization (WHO) modification of

1911the Revised European-American Lymphoma (REAL) classification for the histological

1912classification of Hodgkin lymphoma (HL).[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

19131</a>

1914,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

19152</a>

1916]

1917</p>

1918<section id="_179">

1919<h3 id="_179_toc">

1920WHO Modification of the REAL Classification</h3>

1921<div class="pdq-content-list">

1922<ul id="_9">

1923<li>

1924Classic HL.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1925<ul id="_188" class="list-dash">

1926<li>

1927Nodular sclerosis HL.

1928</li>

1929<li>

1930Mixed-cellularity HL.</li>

1931<li>

1932Lymphocyte-depleted HL.

1933Among 10,019 patients who underwent central expert pathology review for the German Hodgkin Study Group, 84 patients (&lt;1%) were identified as having lymphocyte-depleted classic HL.[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

19343</a>

1935] These patients presented more frequently with advanced-stage HL and <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_966">

1936B symptoms</a>

1937.</li>

1938<li>

1939Lymphocyte-rich classic HL.</li>

1940</ul>

1941</div>

1942</li>

1943<li>

1944Nodular lymphocyte&#8211;predominant HL (NLPHL). NLPHL is a clinicopathological entity of B-cell origin that is distinct from classic HL.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

19454</a>

1946,<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

19475</a>

1948] <p id="_971" tabindex="-1">

1949The typical immunophenotype for classic HL is CD15+, CD20-, CD30+, CD45-, while the profile for lymphocyte-predominant disease is CD15-, CD20+, CD30-, CD45+.</p>

1950</li>

1951</ul>

1952</div>

1982 <section id="_12">

1983

1984 <h2>

1985Stage Information for HL</h2>

1986

1987 <div id="_section_12" class="pdq-sections">

1988<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1989<h6>

1990In This Section</h6>

1991<ul>

1992<li>

1993<a href="#_1153_toc">

1994Staging Subclassification System<ul>

1995<li>

1996<a href="#_739_toc">

1997Lugano Classification</a>

1998</li>

1999</ul>

2000</a>

2001</li>

2002<li>

2003<a href="#_31_toc">

2004Prognostic Groups</a>

2005</li>

2006</ul>

2007</nav>

2008<p id="_477" tabindex="-1">

2009Clinical staging

2010for patients with Hodgkin lymphoma (HL) includes the following: </p>

2011<div class="pdq-content-list">

2012<ul id="_972">

2013<li>

2014Physical examination and history.</li>

2015<li>

2016Laboratory studies (including

2017sedimentation rate).</li>

2018<li>

2019Thoracic and abdominal/pelvic computerized tomographic

2020(CT) scans with or without positron emission tomography (PET).[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

20211</a>

2022] PET scans combined with CT scans have become the standard imaging for clinical staging.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

20232</a>

2024]</li>

2025</ul>

2026</div>

2027<p id="_869" tabindex="-1">

2028Staging laparotomy is no longer recommended and

2029should be considered only when the results will allow substantially less

2030treatment. Staging laparotomy should not be done in patients who require chemotherapy. If the laparotomy is required for treatment decisions, the risks

2031of potential morbidity should be considered.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

20323</a>

2033-<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

20346</a>

2035]</p>

2036<p id="_939" tabindex="-1">

2037Bone marrow involvement occurs in 5% of patients and is more prevalent in the context of constitutional <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_966">

2038B symptoms</a>

2039 and anemia, leukopenia, or thrombocytopenia. In a retrospective review and meta-analysis of 955 patients in nine studies, fewer than 2% of patients with positive bone marrow biopsy results had only stage I or stage II disease on PET-CT scans.[<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

20407</a>

2041] Omission of the bone marrow biopsy for PET-CT&#8211;designated early-stage patients did not change treatment selection.[<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

20427</a>

2043] In addition, focal skeletal bone lesions on PET-CT predicted bone marrow involvement with a 96.9% (93.0%&#8211;99.08%) sensitivity and 99.7% (98.9%&#8211;100%) specificity.[<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

20447</a>

2045] For these reasons, PET-CT has replaced bone marrow biopsy in the clinical staging of newly diagnosed HL.</p>

2046<p id="_940" tabindex="-1">

2047Massive mediastinal disease has been defined by the Cotswolds meeting as a thoracic ratio of maximum transverse mass diameter of 33% or more of the internal transverse thoracic diameter measured at the T5/6 intervertebral disc level on chest radiography.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

20481</a>

2049] Some investigators have designated a lymph node mass measuring 10 cm or more in greatest dimension as massive disease.[<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

20508</a>

2051] Other investigators use a measurement of the maximum width of the mediastinal mass divided by the maximum intrathoracic diameter.[<a href="#cit/section_3.9">

20529</a>

2053]</p>

2054<section id="_1153">

2055<h3 id="_1153_toc">

2056Staging Subclassification System</h3>

2057<section id="_739">

2058<h4 id="_739_toc">

2059Lugano Classification</h4>

2060<p id="_601" tabindex="-1">

2061The American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) has adopted the Lugano classification to evaluate and stage lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

206210</a>

2063] The Lugano classification system replaces the Ann Arbor classification system, which was adopted in 1971 at the Ann Arbor Conference,[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

206411</a>

2065] with some modifications 18 years later from the Cotswolds meeting.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

20661</a>

2067] </p>

2218<p id="_1158" tabindex="-1">

2219The E designation is used when well-localized extranodal lymphoid malignancies

2220arise in or extend to tissues beyond, but near, the major lymphatic aggregates.

2221Stage IV refers to disease that is diffusely spread throughout an extranodal

2222site, such as the liver. If pathological proof of involvement of one or more

2223extralymphatic sites has been documented, the symbol for the site of

2224involvement, followed by a plus sign (+), is listed.</p>

2259<section id="_31">

2260<h3 id="_31_toc">

2261Prognostic Groups</h3>

2262<p id="_873" tabindex="-1">

2263Many investigators and many new clinical trials employ a clinical staging system that divides patients into three major groups that are also useful for the clinician:[<a href="#cit/section_3.12">

226412</a>

2265] </p>

2266<div class="pdq-content-list">

2267<ul id="_973">

2268<li>

2269Early favorable.</li>

2270<li>

2271Early unfavorable.</li>

2272<li>

2273Advanced.</li>

2274</ul>

2275</div>

2276<p id="_974" tabindex="-1">

2277The group assignment depends on:</p>

2278<div class="pdq-content-list">

2279<ul id="_941" class="pdq-address-block">

2280<li>

2281Whether the patient has early or advanced disease.</li>

2282<li>

2283The type and number of adverse prognostic factors present.</li>

2284</ul>

2285</div>

2286<p id="_874" tabindex="-1">

2287<strong>

2288Early-stage adverse prognostic factors:</strong>

2289</p>

2290<div class="pdq-content-list">

2291<ul id="_875">

2292<li>

2293Large mediastinal mass (&gt;33% of the thoracic width on chest x-ray, &#8805;10 cm on CT scan).</li>

2294<li>

2295 Extranodal involvement.</li>

2296<li>

2297 Elevated erythrocyte sedimentation rate (&gt;30 mm/h for <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_966">

2298B stage [symptoms]</a>

2299, &gt;50 mm/h for <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_966">

2300A stage [symptoms]</a>

2301).</li>

2302<li>

2303 Involvement of three or more lymph node areas.</li>

2304<li>

2305 Presence of <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_966">

2306B symptoms</a>

2307.</li>

2308</ul>

2309</div>

2310<p id="_876" tabindex="-1">

2311<strong>

2312Early favorable group:</strong>

2313 Clinical stage I or II without any of the adverse prognostic factors listed above.</p>

2314<p id="_877" tabindex="-1">

2315<strong>

2316Early unfavorable group:</strong>

2317 Clinical stage I or II with one or more of the adverse prognostic factors listed above.</p>

2318<p id="_878" tabindex="-1">

2319<strong>

2320Advanced-stage adverse prognostic factors:</strong>

2321</p>

2322<p id="_879" tabindex="-1">

2323For patients with advanced-stage HL, the International Prognostic Factors Project on Advanced Hodgkin's Disease developed the International Prognostic Index with a score that is based on the following seven adverse prognostic factors:[<a href="#cit/section_3.13">

232413</a>

2325]</p>

2326<div class="pdq-content-list">

2327<ul id="_880">

2328<li>

2329 Albumin level lower than 4.0 g/dL.</li>

2330<li>

2331 Hemoglobin level lower than 10.5 g/dL. </li>

2332<li>

2333 Male sex.</li>

2334<li>

2335 Age 45 years or older.</li>

2336<li>

2337 Stage IV disease.</li>

2338<li>

2339 White blood cell (WBC) count of 15,000/mm<span class="sup">

23403</span>

2341 or higher.</li>

2342<li>

2343 Absolute lymphocytic count lower than 600/mm<span class="sup">

23443</span>

2345 or lymphocyte count higher than 8% of the total WBC count.</li>

2346</ul>

2347</div>

2348<p id="_881" tabindex="-1">

2349<strong>

2350Advanced group:</strong>

2351 Clinical stage III or IV with up to three of the adverse risk factors listed above. Patients with advanced disease have a 60% to 80% rate of freedom from progression of disease at 5 years from treatment with first-line chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.13">

235213</a>

2353][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2354Level of evidence C2</a>

2355] An updated clinical prediction model uses continuous variables listed for the International Prognostic Index above, with an <a href="https://holistic-calculator.web.app/" title="https://holistic-calculator.web.app/">

2356online calculator available</a>

2357.[<a href="#cit/section_3.14">

235814</a>

2359]</p>

2360</section>

2424

2425 <section id="_36">

2426

2427 <h2>

2428Treatment Option Overview for HL</h2>

2429

2430 <div id="_section_36" class="pdq-sections">

2431<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2432<h6>

2433In This Section</h6>

2434<ul>

2435<li>

2436<a href="#_748_toc">

2437Chemotherapy</a>

2438</li>

2439<li>

2440<a href="#_322_toc">

2441Radiation Therapy</a>

2442</li>

2443</ul>

2444</nav>

2445<p id="_38" tabindex="-1">

2446After initial clinical staging for Hodgkin lymphoma (HL), patients with early favorable disease or early unfavorable disease are treated with ABVD (doxorubicin, bleomycin, vinblastine, and dacarbazine) chemotherapy with or without involved-field or nodal radiation.</p>

2447<p id="_998" tabindex="-1">

2448Patients with advanced-stage disease are primarily treated with chemotherapy alone, although subsequent radiation therapy may be applied for initial bulky disease (&#8805;10 cm mediastinal mass) or for residual adenopathy (&gt;2.5 cm) with positive findings after a postchemotherapy positron emission tomography (PET) scan.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

24491</a>

2450] Treatment regimen preferences and application, as well as relative risks, differ regionally.</p>

2451<p id="_747" tabindex="-1">

2452Patients with HL who are older than 60 years may have more treatment-related morbidity and mortality; maintaining the dose intensity of standard chemotherapy may be difficult.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

24532</a>

2454,<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

24553</a>

2456] Other therapies have been proposed for older patients with lower tolerance for conventional regimens, but no randomized trials have been conducted with these regimens.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

24574</a>

2458] Twenty-seven previously untreated patients older than 60 years, judged by the investigator to be in poor condition and unable to undergo chemotherapy, received brentuximab vedotin. A 92% overall response rate and 73% complete remission rate were reported.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

24595</a>

2460][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2461Level of evidence C3</a>

2462] Brentuximab vedotin has been combined with dacarbazine [<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

24636</a>

2464] or sequentially with AVD (doxorubicin, vinblastine, dacarbazine) [<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

24657</a>

2466], reporting acceptable toxicities in an older population. A retrospective review of 287 patients aged 60 years or older with early-stage favorable HL in two German Hodgkin Study Group (GHSG) trials (HD10 and HD13) showed increased bleomycin-induced lung toxicity with more than two cycles of exposure to bleomycin.[<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

24678</a>

2468]</p>

2714<section id="_322">

2715<h3 id="_322_toc">

2716Radiation Therapy</h3>

2717<p id="_323" tabindex="-1">

2718Radiation therapy alone is almost never used to treat patients newly diagnosed with early favorable classic HL.[<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

27199</a>

2720] In HL, the appropriate dose of radiation alone is 20 Gy to 30 Gy to

2721clinically uninvolved sites and 30 Gy to 36 Gy to regions of initial

2722nodal involvement.[<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

27239</a>

2724-<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

272511</a>

2726] When mediastinal radiation will encompass the left side of the heart or will increase breast cancer risk in young female patients, proton therapy may be considered to reduce the radiation dose to organs at risk.[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

272712</a>

2728] When used as a single modality, radiation therapy is delivered to the neck, chest, and axilla (mantle field) and then to an

2729abdominal field to treat para-aortic nodes and the spleen (splenic pedicle). In some patients, pelvic nodes are treated with

2730a third field. The three fields constitute total nodal radiation therapy. In some

2731cases, the pelvic and para-aortic nodes are treated in a single field called an

2732inverted Y.[<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

27339</a>

2734-<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

273511</a>

2736] </p>

2737</section>

Blood 130 (26): 2829-2837, 2017.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=29038340&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=29038340&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2763[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2764</li>

2765<li id="section_4.7">

2766Evens AM, Advani RH, Helenowski IB, et al.: Multicenter Phase II Study of Sequential Brentuximab Vedotin and Doxorubicin, Vinblastine, and Dacarbazine Chemotherapy for Older Patients With Untreated Classical Hodgkin Lymphoma. J Clin Oncol 36 (30): 3015-3022, 2018.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=30179569&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=30179569&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2767[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2768</li>

2769<li id="section_4.8">

Blood 127 (18): 2189-92, 2016.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=26834240&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=26834240&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2771[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2772</li>

2773<li id="section_4.9">

2774Herst J, Crump M, Baldassarre FG, et al.: Management of Early-stage Hodgkin Lymphoma: A Practice Guideline. Clin Oncol (R Coll Radiol) 29 (1): e5-e12, 2017.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27697411&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27697411&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2775[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2776</li>

2777<li id="section_4.10">

2778D&#252;hmke E, Franklin J, Pfreundschuh M, et al.: Low-dose radiation is sufficient for the noninvolved extended-field treatment in favorable early-stage Hodgkin's disease: long-term results of a randomized trial of radiotherapy alone. J Clin Oncol 19 (11): 2905-14, 2001.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=11387364&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=11387364&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2779[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2780</li>

Int J Radiat Oncol Biol Phys 44 (3): 551-61, 1999.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=10348284&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=10348284&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2783[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

Blood 132 (16): 1635-1646, 2018.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=30108066&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=30108066&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2795

2796 <section id="_362">

2797

2798 <h2>

2799Treatment of Early Favorable Classic HL</h2>

2800

2801 <div id="_section_362" class="pdq-sections">

2802<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2803<h6>

2804In This Section</h6>

2805<ul>

2806<li>

2807<a href="#_1143_toc">

2808Treatment Options for Early Favorable Classic HL<ul>

2809<li>

2810<a href="#_922_toc">

2811Chemotherapy with or without radiation therapy</a>

2812</li>

2813</ul>

2814</a>

2815</li>

2816<li>

2817<a href="#_TrialSearch_362_sid_5_toc">

2818Current Clinical Trials</a>

2819</li>

2820</ul>

2821</nav>

2822<p id="_364" tabindex="-1">

2823Patients are designated as having early favorable classic Hodgkin lymphoma (HL) when they have clinical stage I or stage II disease and none of the following adverse prognostic factors:</p>

2824<div class="pdq-content-list">

2825<ul id="_365">

2826<li>

2827B symptoms (unexplained fever &#8805;38&#176;C, soaking night sweats, unexplained weight loss &#8805;10% within 6 months).</li>

2828<li>

2829Extranodal disease.</li>

2830<li>

2831Bulky disease (&#8805;10 cm or &gt;33% of the chest diameter on chest x-ray).</li>

2832<li>

2833Three or more sites of nodal involvement.</li>

2834<li>

2835Sedimentation rate of 50 mm/h or higher.</li>

2836</ul>

2837</div>

2838<section id="_1143">

2839<h3 id="_1143_toc">

2840Treatment Options for Early Favorable Classic HL</h3>

2841<p id="_786" tabindex="-1">

2842Treatment options for early favorable classic HL include the following:</p>

2843<div class="pdq-content-list">

2844<ol id="_921">

2845<li>

2846<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_922">

2847Chemotherapy with or without radiation therapy</a>

2848.</li>

2849</ol>

2850</div>

2851<section id="_922">

2852<h4 id="_922_toc">

2853Chemotherapy with or without radiation therapy</h4>

2854<p id="_923" tabindex="-1">

2855Treatment options include the following:</p>

2856<div class="pdq-content-list">

2857<ul id="_788">

2858<li>

2859ABVD (doxorubicin, bleomycin, vinblastine, and dacarbazine) for three to six cycles.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

28601</a>

2861]</li>

2862<li>

2863ABVD for two to four cycles plus involved-field radiation therapy (IFRT) (20 Gy or 30 Gy).</li>

2864<li>

2865Radiation therapy alone in certain circumstances (such as for older adults with absolute contraindications for using chemotherapy).[<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

28662</a>

2867,<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

28683</a>

2869]</li>

2870</ul>

2871</div>

2872<p id="_366" tabindex="-1">

2873Historically, radiation therapy alone was the primary treatment for patients with early favorable classic HL, often after confirmatory negative staging laparotomy.</p>

2874<p id="_951" tabindex="-1">

2875The late mortality from solid tumors (especially in the lung, breast, gastrointestinal tract, and connective tissue) and cardiovascular disease makes radiation therapy a less-attractive option for the best-risk patients, who have the highest probability of cure and long-term survival.[<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

28764</a>

2877-<a href="#cit/section_5.8">

28788</a>

2879] Clinical trials have focused on regimens with chemotherapy and IFRT or with chemotherapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

28801</a>

2881]</p>

2882<p id="_790" tabindex="-1">

2883Evidence (chemotherapy and/or radiation therapy):</p>

2884<p id="_1095" tabindex="-1">

2885For patients with early favorable classic HL, the following four trials established ABVD alone for four cycles or ABVD for two cycles plus 20 Gy of IFRT.</p>

2886<div class="pdq-content-list">

2887<ol id="_791">

2888<li>

2889A randomized, prospective trial from the National Cancer Institute of Canada involving 123 patients with early favorable classic HL compared ABVD for four to six cycles with subtotal nodal radiation.[<a href="#cit/section_5.9">

28909</a>

2891][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2892Level of evidence A1</a>

2893]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2894<ul id="_793">

2895<li>

2896With a median follow-up of 11.3 years, no difference was observed in event-free survival rates (89% vs. 86%; <em>

2897P</em>

2898 = .64) or in overall survival rates (OS) (98% vs. 98%; <em>

2899P</em>

2900 = .95).</li>

2901</ul>

2902</div>

2903</li>

2904<li>

2905A randomized study from the Milan Cancer Institute of patients with clinical early-stage HL compared 4 months of ABVD followed by IFRT with 4 months of ABVD followed by extended-field radiation therapy (EFRT).[<a href="#cit/section_5.10">

290610</a>

2907][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2908Level of evidence B1</a>

2909]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2910<ul id="_864">

2911<li>

2912The results showed similar OS and freedom from progression of disease with a 10-year median follow-up, but the study had inadequate statistical power to determine noninferiority of IFRT versus EFRT.</li>

2913</ul>

2914</div>

2915</li>

2916<li>

2917In the HD10 trial, the German Hodgkin Study Group (GHSG) randomly assigned 1,190 patients with early favorable HL to receive one of the following:[<a href="#cit/section_5.11">

291811</a>

2919,<a href="#cit/section_5.12">

292012</a>

2921][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2922Level of evidence A1</a>

2923]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2924<ul id="_1148">

2925<li>

2926Two cycles of ABVD plus 30 Gy of IFRT.</li>

2927<li>

2928Two cycles of ABVD plus 20 Gy of IFRT.</li>

2929<li>

2930Four cycles of ABVD plus 30 Gy of IFRT.</li>

2931<li>

2932Four cycles of ABVD plus 20 Gy of IFRT.

2933 </li>

2934</ul>

2935</div>

2936<p id="_1096" tabindex="-1">

2937The following results were observed for the trial:</p>

2938<div class="pdq-content-list">

2939<ul id="_1097">

2940<li>

2941With an 8.2-year median follow-up, no differences were observed (hazard ratio, 1.0; 95% CI, 0.6&#8211;1.5) in 10-year progression-free survival (PFS) rates (87%) or OS rates (94%) for all four groups.</li>

2942</ul>

2943</div>

2944</li>

2945<li>

2946A follow-up study by the GHSG compared modified versions of ABVD with elimination of dacarbazine, bleomycin, or both in combination with 30 Gy of radiation therapy in 1,502 patients with early favorable HL.[<a href="#cit/section_5.13">

294713</a>

2948] <div class="pdq-content-list">

2949<ul id="_1125">

2950<li>

2951After 5 years, freedom from treatment failure was significantly worse when dacarbazine, bleomycin, or both were omitted. </li>

2952<li>

2953This trial suggests that ABVD remains the standard chemotherapy regimen.</li>

2954</ul>

2955</div>

2956</li>

2957</ol>

2958</div>

2959<p id="_1000" tabindex="-1">

2960Other trials have investigated the role of positron emission tomography (PET) scans for early favorable HL.</p>

2961<div class="pdq-content-list">

2962<ol id="_1126">

2963<li>

2964Three prospective randomized trials of 2,889 patients with early-stage disease investigated the use of PET&#8210;computed tomography (CT) scans to modify therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

296514</a>

2966-<a href="#cit/section_5.16">

296716</a>

2968]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2969<ul id="_1127">

2970<li>

2971Among patients with early favorable HL who had negative PET-CT scan results (Deauville score of 1 or 2) after two or three cycles of ABVD, radiation therapy could be omitted with no significant loss of PFS or OS.[<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

297214</a>

2973-<a href="#cit/section_5.16">

297416</a>

2975][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2976Level of evidence B1</a>

2977]</li>

2978<li>

2979ABVD was given for three cycles (six doses) in the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00943423">

2980RAPID</a>

2981 study (NCT00943423),[<a href="#cit/section_5.15">

298215</a>

2983] for four cycles (eight doses) in the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00433433">

2984EORTC H10F</a>

2985 study (NCT00433433),[<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

298614</a>

2987] and for two cycles (four doses) in the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00736320">

2988GHSG HD16</a>

2989 study (NCT00736320) [<a href="#cit/section_5.16">

299016</a>

2991] when applied without radiation therapy.</li>

2992<li>

2993None of the studies randomly assigned therapy for positive results from an interim PET-CT scan (Deauville score of 3, 4, or 5) after two or three cycles of ABVD because this occurred in only 15% to 25% of the patients studied. One of the studies (RAPID) added an extra cycle of ABVD and IFRT to 30 Gy,[<a href="#cit/section_5.15">

299415</a>

2995] another study (EORTC H10F) switched to BEACOPP (bleomycin, etoposide, doxorubicin, cyclophosphamide, vincristine, procarbazine, and prednisone)&#8211;escalated therapy for two cycles plus involved nodal radiation therapy to 30 Gy,[<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

299614</a>

2997] and the other study (GHSG HD16) added IFRT to 30 Gy.[<a href="#cit/section_5.16">

299816</a>

2999]<p id="_1252" tabindex="-1">

3000In the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00943423">

3001RAPID</a>

3002 study (NCT00943423), patients with postchemotherapy PET-CT Deauville scores of 5 (uptake &#8805;3 times maximum liver uptake) had inferior 5-year PFS rates (61.9%; 95% CI, 41.1%&#8211;82.7%) and 5-year OS rates (85.2%; 95% CI, 69.7%&#8211;100%) (<em>

3003P</em>

3004 = .002) when compared with patients with Deauville scores of 1 to 4 (<em>

3005P</em>

3006 &lt; .001).[<a href="#cit/section_5.17">

300717</a>

3008]</p>

3009</li>

3010</ul>

3011</div>

3012</li>

3013</ol>

3014</div>

3015<p id="_1001" tabindex="-1">

3016<strong>

3017Older patients with early favorable HL have also been studied.</strong>

3018</p>

3019<div class="pdq-content-list">

3020<ol id="_1128">

3021<li>

3022In 287 patients older than 60 years or with early favorable disease, a retrospective review of pulmonary toxicity in the HD10 and HD13 trials showed the following:[<a href="#cit/section_5.18">

302318</a>

3024]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3025<ul id="_1129">

3026<li>

3027Two cycles of ABVD plus IFRT (137 patients): 2% pulmonary toxicity.</li>

3028<li>

3029Two cycles of AVD (omitting bleomycin) plus IFRT (82 patients): 2% pulmonary toxicity.</li>

3030<li>

3031Four cycles of ABVD plus IFRT (68 patients): 10% pulmonary toxicity.</li>

3032</ul>

3033</div>

3034</li>

3035</ol>

3036</div>

3037<p id="_1005" tabindex="-1">

3038<strong>

3039For older patients (&gt;60 years) with early favorable disease, when more than two cycles of ABVD are required, bleomycin may be omitted to avoid pulmonary toxicity.</strong>

3040</p>

3041<p id="_1241" tabindex="-1">

3042<strong>

3043Summary of early favorable classic HL:</strong>

3044</p>

3045<div class="pdq-content-list">

3046<ul id="_1007">

3047<li>

3048ABVD alone for three to four cycles is recommended for patients with early favorable classical HL when the interim PET-CT scan results are negative after two or three cycles of chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_5.19">

304919</a>

3050] These patients are also unlikely to ever have a relapse, so routine CT scans are not recommended in <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq#_970">

3051follow-up</a>

3052.</li>

3053<li>

3054With positive interim PET-CT scan results, extra cycles of ABVD and involved nodal radiation therapy are recommended.</li>

3055<li>

3056A combined-modality approach with two cycles of ABVD and 20 Gy of IFRT can also be used for patients with early favorable classic HL.[<a href="#cit/section_5.19">

305719</a>

3058] In this situation, a PET-CT scan to assess response after completion of therapy would suffice.</li>

3059</ul>

3060</div>

3061</section>

3062</section>

3063<section id="_TrialSearch_362_sid_5">

3064<h3 id="_TrialSearch_362_sid_5_toc">

3065Current Clinical Trials</h3>

3066<p id="_TrialSearch_362_22" tabindex="-1">

3067Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

3068advanced clinical trial search</a>

3069 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3070General information</a>

3071 about clinical trials is also available.</p>
